  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b3c952ac-ad59-495e-9412-d70010d0e653'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12709848472083230438"><i>“In the summer of 1947, when the creation of the state of Pakistan was formally announced, ten million people—Muslims and Hindus and Sikhs—were in flight. By the time the monsoon broke, almost a million of them were dead, and all of northern India was in arms, in terror, or in hiding. The only remaining oases of peace were a scatter of little villages lost in the remote re</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12709848472083230438" style="display:none"><i>“In the summer of 1947, when the creation of the state of Pakistan was formally announced, ten million people—Muslims and Hindus and Sikhs—were in flight. By the time the monsoon broke, almost a million of them were dead, and all of northern India was in arms, in terror, or in hiding. The only remaining oases of peace were a scatter of little villages lost in the remote reaches of the frontier. One of these villages was Mano Majra.”</i><br /><br />It is a place, Khushwant Singh goes on to tell us at the beginning of this classic novel, where Sikhs and Muslims have lived together in peace for hundreds of years. Then one day, at the end of the summer, the “ghost train” arrives, a silent, incredible funeral train loaded with the bodies of thousands of refugees, bringing the village its first taste of the horrors of the civil war. Train to Pakistan is the story of this isolated village that is plunged into the abyss of religious hate. It is also the story of a Sikh boy and a Muslim girl whose love endured and transcends the ravages of war.<br /><br />Introduction by Arthur Lall</span>
  <a data-text-id="12709848472083230438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It was definitely Jugga. The man who cut the rope is described to be a "big" man and Iqbal was anything but big. Earlier in the book Iqbal is describe<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It was definitely Jugga. The man who cut the rope is described to be a "big" man and Iqbal was anything but big. Earlier in the book Iqbal is described to be "small and somewhat effeminate".<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I feel it's more of a general comment than a question. Yes the story isn't exactly new if you consider the writings of the same genre by many other au<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I feel it's more of a general comment than a question. Yes the story isn't exactly new if you consider the writings of the same genre by many other authors at that time, but personally speaking, Singh's storytelling is totally of a different level. I haven't read all other relevant works, if course; but you know an exceptional work when you read one.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_254727097" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254727097" />
    <a title="Srinivas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6716777-srinivas"><img alt="Srinivas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552030942p2/6716777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254727097?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Srinivas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Srinivas" href="/user/show/6716777-srinivas">Srinivas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254727097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9827540811920531618">every time i want to write a review, i just, struck, plain and simple. but this time i decided to write anything or something. <br/><br/>why i gave this book five stars?. because its an Indian literature? and about us- Indians?. no, certainly not, because its about characters, which are, u know, are fictitious, but situated in in non-fictitious and hard-core reality, struggle to maintain balance consistently between whats good and bad. <br/><br/><b>Train to Pakistan by Khushwant Singh</b> is a story about the violence dur</span>
  <span id="freeText9827540811920531618" style="display:none">every time i want to write a review, i just, struck, plain and simple. but this time i decided to write anything or something. <br /><br />why i gave this book five stars?. because its an Indian literature? and about us- Indians?. no, certainly not, because its about characters, which are, u know, are fictitious, but situated in in non-fictitious and hard-core reality, struggle to maintain balance consistently between whats good and bad. <br /><br /><b>Train to Pakistan by Khushwant Singh</b> is a story about the violence during Hindustan and Pakistan creation(1947). so naturally we can assume it as a story about Hindu-Muslim communal violence, where there are some good people and bad people in both parties, some heroes and some villains also there, good people trying to save good people from opposite side, people turning bad towards their own side people. <br /><br />In between all this chaos, <br /><br />there was<br /><br />a character, who was a magistrate, held high position, not by politically, but socially in the minds of illiterate and socially under achievers, but good people. He picked a sixteen or less years old girl (she herself not sure about her age) to have physical sex and she reminded him as his dead daughter and he drove it from his conscious mind by drinking whiskey. This man became responsible to save hundreds of people (not related to him by blood, caste, religion and by any other social means except they are humans) and to stop to turn a train into corpse carrier.<br /><br />a dacoit socially a wrong man and morally good one, fallen love with a girl of opposite side(according situation the, one side had to kill opposite side), also pregnant-ed her, and also sacrificed himself(physically means killed) to save her and to stop to turn a train into corpse carrier.<br /><br />a comrade, atheist, Muslim by religion preached about morals highly, about government's inability, about rich and poor and he also morally good and but failed to do anything to stop to turn a train into corpse carrier. <br /><br />a river, its water increasing, crossing its borders and ready to eat villages, standing beside her(its metaphor, i think, indicating growing evil)<br /><br />Khushwant sir put all these things and several others, transformed it into a black-humored, suspense story(because till the end i don't is the turn going to turn a train into corpse carrier? i wished Train to be turned into train into corpse carrier and then its give an another reason simply to dislike the religion, and i dislike religion) about some people living in a village, beside Hindustan and Pakistan borders, not affected by Hindustan and Pakistan splitting-hating-raping-butchering reality, but going to be affected soon and about the trains were carrying corpses which were crossing from both countries to respective countries. <br /><br />Khushwant sir given a clear picture of social, political, physical and mental atmospheres, during that period,of people who must be happy that they got freedom, instead they like to cleanse opposite side of their respective homes.<br /><br /><br /><br />i don't know its a review or something or anything. just wanted to write something or anything about this book.<br /><br />if anyone read this review or something or anything, please not forget to mention grammar mistakes, know i did but don't know where</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25605420-apoorva?shelf=beautiful-writing">beautiful-writing</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2600376704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9102359525226462874"><a href='https://eternaldreamlander.wordpress.com/2018/12/28/train-to-pakistan-by-khushwant-singh/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Read on blog</a><br/><br/>The story takes place in an isolated village Mano Majra where people from different religions like Hindus, Sikhs, and Muslims co-exist in peace. The village stands as a symbol of ignorance and peace until the harmony is threatened by an incident which raises suspicion, chaos, and animosity that ensues from the rift created among different communities.<br/><br/>The story is told from the point of view of three major characters: Jagga, an infamous robber, Hukum Chand, a magistrate who’s responsi</span>
  <span id="freeText9102359525226462874" style="display:none"><a href="https://eternaldreamlander.wordpress.com/2018/12/28/train-to-pakistan-by-khushwant-singh/" rel="nofollow noopener">Read on blog</a><br /><br />The story takes place in an isolated village Mano Majra where people from different religions like Hindus, Sikhs, and Muslims co-exist in peace. The village stands as a symbol of ignorance and peace until the harmony is threatened by an incident which raises suspicion, chaos, and animosity that ensues from the rift created among different communities.<br /><br />The story is told from the point of view of three major characters: Jagga, an infamous robber, Hukum Chand, a magistrate who’s responsible for the peace in the village and Iqbal, a political activist and a mysterious identity who visits the village with a purpose of triggering proletariat revolution.<br /><br />Despite having a slow pace, the book is very engaging. The scenic descriptions and the depiction of the daily lives of the people in the book are so vivid and detailed, it feels like I was present there and experiencing things myself.<br /><br />I loved the use of symbolism to depict the events happening in the village. The disruption of the day-to-day activities like the train schedule shows the disruption of the rhythm of their daily lives.<br /> <br />The author, at various places, criticizes the different aspects of society like people’s moral codes, religious beliefs, inequality, corrupt practices, and points out the hypocrisy of the people. The situation in the village shows how easy it is to manipulate people on religious grounds.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Since they abused and beat him without anger or hate, they were not human beings with names. They were only denominations one tried to get the better of. If one failed, it was just bad luck.</b>
</i><br /><br />The ending just brought tears to my eyes. The author neatly depicts the complexity of human nature and points out the difference between one’s beliefs and actions. The book stays relevant even now as things haven’t changed much in today’s age. It’s a beautiful book worth reading.</span>

<div id="review_3437199997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3437199997" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535136437p2/3536004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3437199997?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenna by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/97513431-tamoghna-biswas">Tamoghna Biswas</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=historical-fic">historical-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classic-of-the-month">classic-of-the-month</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=out-of-asia">out-of-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3437199997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8843222368785589779">Once again, Goodreads proves to be the perfect place to find books. <i>Train to Pakistan</i> is a book I probably would never have learned about, or considered reading if I did, had it not been for a friend's review of this classic novel.<br/><br/>It is set during India's Partition, in a small, fictional village. For hundreds of years, Sikhs and Muslims have lived together peacefully. <br/><br/>We witness on a small scale the inter-religious hatred and violence that tore apart India in the 1940s. <br/><br/>When reading about histor</span>
  <span id="freeText8843222368785589779" style="display:none">Once again, Goodreads proves to be the perfect place to find books. <i>Train to Pakistan</i> is a book I probably would never have learned about, or considered reading if I did, had it not been for a friend's review of this classic novel.<br /><br />It is set during India's Partition, in a small, fictional village. For hundreds of years, Sikhs and Muslims have lived together peacefully. <br /><br />We witness on a small scale the inter-religious hatred and violence that tore apart India in the 1940s. <br /><br />When reading about historical events, we can sometimes forget that real people lived through them. We see them from afar and through the long lens of history. We are divorced from the personal struggles and suffering, see the tragedy on a large scale instead of from the point of view of the people.<br /><br />In <i>Train to Pakistan</i>, author Khushwant Singh takes us into the heart of the Partition. We witness history from the perspective of the individual, see how easily hatred and discord can spread. We observe how fear of the other proliferates, how easily people can be coerced to turn on their neighbours. <br /><br />Singh introduces several characters to us and writes from their various points of view. This made the story all the more real as we see these people in all their humanity, good and bad. The characters are complicated and well-fleshed out. Some you feel compassion for, others disgust at their mistreatment of others. There is an educated social reformer with false accusations against him, a young Muslim woman in love with a Sikh man, bandits, and corrupt policemen. Normal people going about their lives, thinking their small village was safe from what was happening in the rest of India.<br /><br />When a train full of corpses arrives, we see how quickly fear spreads and how quickly people can be swayed by rumors and begin to distrust people they've known their entire lives.<br /><br />This is a tragic story, dark and dismal... and yet brilliant. It is a book I will not soon forget. <br /><br /><br /><i>(August 2020 classic-of-the-month)</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8843222368785589779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_185175440" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185175440" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4349787-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552437135p2/4349787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185175440?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/4349787-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788174364449" href="/book/show/518361.Train_to_Pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4349787-rachel?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185175440" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14415603331126802294">This book, particularly this version with photographs from Margaret Bourke-White (a pioneer in photojournalism) was fantastic. It is short but a powerful story about the Partition of India in 1947—an event I am sorry to say I had not known much about until coming on this field study to India. I began it on my own train ride to Pakistan.<br/><br/>Okay, so maybe not Pakistan, but a train to Amritsar and the Pakistan border. That has to count for something, right?<br/><br/>Reading this during that experience both impa</span>
  <span id="freeText14415603331126802294" style="display:none">This book, particularly this version with photographs from Margaret Bourke-White (a pioneer in photojournalism) was fantastic. It is short but a powerful story about the Partition of India in 1947—an event I am sorry to say I had not known much about until coming on this field study to India. I began it on my own train ride to Pakistan.<br /><br />Okay, so maybe not Pakistan, but a train to Amritsar and the Pakistan border. That has to count for something, right?<br /><br />Reading this during that experience both impacted my experience and my reading. The Indian train system itself is something to marvel at. When this still functioning colonial train first pulled up to the station I just stared. People hanging out of windows, out of the open door frames, crammed to the roof. I think that Khushwant Singh’s description was remarkably parallel to my journey. Compartments made for fifty with “almost two hundred people, sitting on the floor, on seats, on luggage racks, on trunks, on bed rolls, and on each other,” the oppressive “heat and smell… tempers frayed [because] someone had spread himself out too much or had trod on another’s foot on the way to the lavatory” (59). All of the above happened on the short two hour crawl to Amritsar. <br /><br />But as I started to read more I thought more about my first train ride. Amritsar probably had the highest volumes of trains come through filled with dead corpses from both sides of the Pakistan border. Could my train, my very seat, once been full of dead bodies?<br /><br />My experience in Amritsar was probably most profoundly altered from this reading at the Indian Pakistan border. Only 30 minutes away from the Golden Temple, it is something of a tourist attraction. There are two triumphant looking arches on both sides and two gates in between the two dividing the countries. Each side has a few rows of hot cement stadium seating for people to come and observe the lowering of the flags ceremony conducted by the military. People were taking turns running their countries flags to the middle gates and back, blazing their own music, and having competitions to see who could hold out the longest shout. At the bottom of the stadium on the Indian side there were people dancing and screaming, “Hindustan, Hindustan!” Some of my friends joined them. I stayed in my seat.<br /><br />I couldn’t get over the fact that we were celebrating this great divide. The ceremonies were identical on either side. Everything seemed the same expect for one thing. One big thing.<br /><br />Religion.<br /><br />The flags were lowered, a handshake was exchanged between the guards, and the gates were slammed shut for the day. I do not know enough about what has happened since Partition when this book was set to understand what the current situation is between these two countries with a rocky history together, but in the moment I just could not stop thinking about this book. Why are we celebrating? Or is it time to stop crying over spilled milk?<br /><br />The themes of this book crept up again during my experience in Amritsar. We went to one restaurant and the owner was sat beneath three pictures—one of Lord Shiva, one of Guru Nanak, and the third of his late father. One of the girls in our group straight up asked him if he was a Hindu. He gave her a blank look and held up his middle finger, “there is only one God.” I get that the middle finger means something different in this context, but I thought there was a rich irony to that in a location that probably has not scarred over from a very recent wound. What is the legacy of Partition in India today, especially here on the border?<br /><br />The end message of this book was profound. It is individuals who change the world. You don’t have to be a politician, have twenty degrees, a strong religious background, or even a lot of friends or support; you just have to make the right decision even when no one else will stand up with you. <br /><br />Pictures of my experience can be found on my academic blog <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://rachelspassagetoindia.blogspot.com/2011/07/my-experience-at-indian-pakistan-border.html">http://rachelspassagetoindia.blogspot...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14415603331126802294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1865168876?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samra Yusuf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samra Yusuf" href="/user/show/56939723-samra-yusuf">Samra Yusuf</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56939723-samra-yusuf?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56939723-samra-yusuf?shelf=asian">asian</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1865168876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1854319850114790363">It is interesting to read historical novels where history is written as fiction.<br/>Here is a novel that tries to record a memorable phase of Indian history through fictional route but the essence is hardly lost in the process.....<br/>We come to know a village at brink of Sutlej that is littered with equal majority of Sikhs and Muslims living together since a long past ago.That is the time of partition, the summer of 1947. Muslims leave India and their exodus to Pakistan is not free from human suffering</span>
  <span id="freeText1854319850114790363" style="display:none">It is interesting to read historical novels where history is written as fiction.<br />Here is a novel that tries to record a memorable phase of Indian history through fictional route but the essence is hardly lost in the process.....<br />We come to know a village at brink of Sutlej that is littered with equal majority of Sikhs and Muslims living together since a long past ago.That is the time of partition, the summer of 1947. Muslims leave India and their exodus to Pakistan is not free from human sufferings.and so is the case with Hindus and Sikhs leaving Pakistan.<br />Story is one very simple in nature if viewed as a love story of a Sikh Scoundrel and a Muslim girl, that ends tragically as the hero sacrifices his own life to save that of her beloved(typical eastern acclaim of "true-love")<br />but,if viewed from a broader canvass,Mano Majra is the one main character that influences the whole lot of events that follows and lead us to other more lucidly placed characters..<br />We come to learn the psyche of A village Magistrate,his convictions and conventions,his conflicts and confusions,his resolutions and improvisations his conscience leaden with sins and his Morales of a saint!<br />And we come to know the head of Sikh temple,Meet Singh naive in manners but very a pacifist in thoughts,his appearance is of a village-dweller but his sayings outwit the so-called western-returned Babu Sahib.<br />Babu Sahib,the spokesman of author,plays a role of a critique,his name alone is an ambivalence for others, He is a rebel, sentimentally attracted to Socialist thinking, but he is primarily concerned with personal leadership. The central quality of Iqbal is that he does not belong anywhere and, what is worse, he pathetically desires to contribute to the mass upsurge of India’s rural committees. When the time comes for his action, he does nothing.(nobody seems to does nothing when needed desperately)<br />Singh has a simple way of telling complicated things,The novel has simple text, not so long-winded in sentence structures, easy to go through and especially lucid for those readers that resist a large mass of text and labyrinthine plots.a dozen more characters,some violence,mindlessness,sex,bigotry,and more violence.<br />But no doubt,author weaves a heart-wrenching story in the backdrop of Partition..<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1854319850114790363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1622862228" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1622862228" />
    <a title="Yamna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22400969-yamna"><img alt="Yamna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588864877p2/22400969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1622862228?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yamna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yamna" href="/user/show/22400969-yamna">Yamna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1622862228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4344021758256062075"><i> 14 August and 15 August- Independence Day of two extremely opposite yet completely alike countries, once a single entity, now torn brothers </i> <br/><br/><i> <b> I am the offspring of blood and pain.<br/>I am the wind heavy with the stench of fear on the eve of 14 August 1947. I am the screams of protest, I am the fury that binds me to this soil, every night, every year.<br/>I am the worthless sacrifices that blended into the earth so well, you walk those same paths and you never see the shadows of the bones hovering over </b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText4344021758256062075" style="display:none"><i> 14 August and 15 August- Independence Day of two extremely opposite yet completely alike countries, once a single entity, now torn brothers </i> <br /><br /><i> <b> I am the offspring of blood and pain.<br />I am the wind heavy with the stench of fear on the eve of 14 August 1947. I am the screams of protest, I am the fury that binds me to this soil, every night, every year.<br />I am the worthless sacrifices that blended into the earth so well, you walk those same paths and you never see the shadows of the bones hovering over every stone. <br />I am the ball of gutless strength, I tore flesh apart, I made tendons burst because every man who dares leave is the father of shame. <br />I am the fortress of hope, I made people flee under the midnight-black sky. <br />I am the space between solitude and sorrow, I witnessed children’s bodies displayed on the edges of swords, a prize worth every ounce of joy.<br />I am the whisper in the night, the one that jerks you awake, as you recollect memories of escaping a fate worse than death. <br />I have the blood of the innocent on my arms, a tattoo I must wear; I shall never forget it as I move on from the despair I caused people <br />I am the result of a massacre, I am the result of a struggle, I am the result of an effort that blinded the world. <br />I am the impossible that happened after decades of vain desires. <br />I am the fire, I burn within every survivor. I am the follower of peace, I worship it to make myself feel like I did not cause pain.<br />I am the soul of soldiers, the master of endurance, the dignity men and women lost while bound in a train oozing with corpses. <br />I am the forces that helped bring to life a country that never forgot, a state that sustained, and an odd against all odds. <br />I am fury, I am misery, I am powerful, I am pure, I am tainted, I am lost.<br />I am the mother of these winds, I am the child of these lands, I am the father of these fountains of courage. <br />I survived a partition that made humanity question itself. I am every loving parent that is a murderer, I made people commit unspeakable acts in the name of faith. <br />I exist, I nurture, I give, I take. <br />Watch me, world, I am brimming with the descendants of victims and convicts. I rose from the ashes of those I helped burn and those I watched catch flames from the burning lands left after a cruel battle. <br />I am a giant, I am a tiny flame. <br />I am a legacy, I am a legend. <br />The world must not forget.<br />Come for me, come conquer me if you can. <br />I am my people, my people have survived. And those who survive are not afraid to fight.<br />I am my country. <br />My blood is tinted green, my heart is matted with white veins. </b><br /><br />By the summer of 1947, when the creation of the new state of Pakistan was formally announced, ten million people—Muslims and Hindus and Sikhs—were in flight. By the time the monsoon broke, almost a million of them were dead, and all of northern India was in arms, in terror, or in hiding. The only remaining oases of peace were a scatter of little villages lost in the remote reaches of the frontier. </i> <br /><br />And thus begin the journey that will mark one of the biggest massacres in history, one of the most harrowing tales of separation of two countries and an enmity that will blossom instead of wither in the next 70 years.<br /><br />Train to Pakistan is one of those few efforts to save what little love is left between India and Pakistan. I went into it with an open mind; I knew there would be a few points where the age-old debate of which party was right will come up but I was surprised by how the book took a turn for the better, instead of for the worse. <br />In four chapters, Khushwant Singh weaves the stories of one Sikh <i> lambardar </i> keeping peace in the little village, Mano Majra, where Sikhs, Muslims and one Hindu live in peace, as brothers, one village <i> badmash </i> wrongfully convicted of a murder and courting a Muslim girl, one Sikh with no religion and no beliefs, sent to help the villagers learn of what was happening after the partition and one government employee, waddling between the right and wrong decisions. <br />In a series of events, we learn of how one train’s arrival changes the views of the villagers and makes friends turn into unlikely foe. Relationships are tested, those in power manipulate those with no sense of right, and one man’s words bind the reader into a story full of so much pain, it ached to end the book. Train to Pakistan is not a light read; it wasn’t meant to be. It was written to show the slaughter of innocents that took place after the partition and how the violence was never needed, but we were made to believe that it was. <br />The two lines that perfectly describe why all those killings were done are as follows:<br /><br /><i> What have our tenants done?’<br />‘They are Muslims.’ </i><br />The same went for the Sikhs. What had they done? They belonged to a different religion. People with the capability of surviving as brothers chose to kill in the name of religion. They still do. <br />Nothing has changed. But so has everything.<br /><br /><i> <b> Not forever does the bulbul sing<br />In balmy shades of bowers,<br />Not forever lasts the spring<br />Nor ever blossom flowers.<br />Not forever reigneth joy,<br />Sets the sun on days of bliss,<br />Friendships not forever last,<br />They know not life, who know not this.<br />However, there is still hope for us. </b> </i> <br />I could go on and on about how I, as a Muslim, faced difficulties from Hindus or vice versa. But what good would that do? Just like the bulbul with its time-bound songs, there is a season for everything; happiness and sadness. For every Hindu that has killed my people or discriminated, there has been a Muslim that did the same. And, somehow, we forgot to see how we are so similar, not just in our views but also in our actions. I wake up every day, I eat my desi nashta and so does the person living in the country next to me. We have similar functions, similar cultures, similar festivals, yet we choose to fight over something so trivial.<br />We even have the same religious teachings:<br /><br />From the Sikh Scripture<br /><br /><i> <b> Air, water and earth,<br />Of these are we made,<br />Air like the Guru’s word gives the breath of life<br />To the babe born of the great mother Earth<br />Sired by the waters. </b> </i><br /><br />From the Quran<br /><br /><b> <i> In the name of thy Lord and Cherisher<br />Who created<br />Created man from a (mere) clot of congealed blood<br />Your Lord is Most Generous. <br />Who taught knowledge by the pen<br />Taught man what he did not know </i> </b><br /><br />Today, in the 21st Century, I still feel the glimpses of everything that made humans act as monsters in many eyes. I shudder to think of a repetition of those events. But then I wonder. Everything is how we see it. And everything is how we <i> choose </i> to see it. Hence, those words at the start. I choose to see peace, I choose to see friendship. And, I choose to see harmony between Pakistan and India one day. Because<br /><br /><i> Within myself have I lit a fire<br />That now robs me of my breath.<br />The nights I spend in counting stars,<br />The days in dreams of days to be </i><br /><br /><b> I am my country. <br />My blood is tinted green, my heart is matted with white veins </b><br /><br />I was born in a country made in the name of love, loyalty and faith. And making it a country that fulfils these principles is what should be our main goal.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4344021758256062075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2393136729?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2393136729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9209266465187783172"> Train to Pakistan is a superb book on many levels. It is a documentary of Punjab, its people, its culture. Its a narrative of the gruesome events that burned northern India in 1947. It is a story of the cultural, political, and intellectual atmosphere of India at the time. And it succeeds BRILLIANTLY. It brings the reader into the picture so vividly, its rather disturbing. If the reader is a product of the society the athor writes about, or is intimately familiar with it, and possesses any amou</span>
  <span id="freeText9209266465187783172" style="display:none"> Train to Pakistan is a superb book on many levels. It is a documentary of Punjab, its people, its culture. Its a narrative of the gruesome events that burned northern India in 1947. It is a story of the cultural, political, and intellectual atmosphere of India at the time. And it succeeds BRILLIANTLY. It brings the reader into the picture so vividly, its rather disturbing. If the reader is a product of the society the athor writes about, or is intimately familiar with it, and possesses any amount of intellectual spark, this book is an absolute must read. How much it'll mean to you if you are not familiar with the culture of Punjab, I don't know. </span>

<div id="review_2007367032" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2007367032" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2007367032?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2007367032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17106854842003484471">3 June 1947 – Viceroy Mountbatten announces the plan for the Partition of India into two independent states. Independence Day for the new countries will be 14 and 15 August.<br/><br/>Journalist : <i>Do you foresee any mass transfer of population? </i><br/><br/>Viceroy Mountbatten : <i>Personally, I don’t see it. </i><br/><br/>Why did Jinnah and Nehru accede to such an insane, precipitous, huggermugger plan?<br/><br/><i>The truth is that we were tired men and we were getting on in years…the plan for partition offered a way out and we took it.</i> – Nehru i</span>
  <span id="freeText17106854842003484471" style="display:none">3 June 1947 – Viceroy Mountbatten announces the plan for the Partition of India into two independent states. Independence Day for the new countries will be 14 and 15 August.<br /><br />Journalist : <i>Do you foresee any mass transfer of population? </i><br /><br />Viceroy Mountbatten : <i>Personally, I don’t see it. </i><br /><br />Why did Jinnah and Nehru accede to such an insane, precipitous, huggermugger plan?<br /><br /><i>The truth is that we were tired men and we were getting on in years…the plan for partition offered a way out and we took it.</i> – Nehru in 1960<br /><br />Yasmin Khan, from her book <i>The Great Partition:<br /><br />By August 1947 all the ingredients were in place for ethnic cleansing in Punjab: a feeble and polarized police force, the steady withdrawal of British troops and their substitution with the limited and undermanned Punjab Boundary Forces and a petrified and well-armed population. <br /><br />If your home fell on the wrong side of the border when it was finally announced, many argued, you would not be living as a minority in a modern, democratic nation state. Instead, you would suffer oppression, exploitation, the dishonoring of religion and perhaps even conversion or death. </i><br /><br />As I said in my review of that book, the flight of millions of refugees from or to Pakistan happened without state intervention – both of the brand new states had hardly any military available to them to enforce any controls whatsoever. This was unplanned chaos fuelled by self-fulfulling convictions of the worst version of what people are like. This was stampeding for the exit. The number of deaths was never established. Between one and two million, probably.<br /><br />Khushwant Singh takes all this complexity and organises it into 180 perfectly controlled and orchestrated pages. He is not overwhelmed although his readers may well be as they are whirled along in this dance of death. <br /><br />Recommended.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17106854842003484471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3552915490" />
    <a title="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90347401-elsa-rajan-pradhananga"><img alt="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595732933p2/90347401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3552915490?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" href="/user/show/90347401-elsa-rajan-pradhananga">Elsa Rajan Pradhananga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3552915490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2546118776720625553">The darkest period in Indian history coincided with the time India attained independence, because communal violence that broke out as a result of the partition of the country. Those on the wrong side of the Radcliffe line paid the price as rioters abducted, tortured, raped, killed and looted. Khushwant Singh's <i>Train to Pakistan</i> is a novel set in a fictional town Mano Majra where Muslims and Sikhs coexisted in peace, unaware of India's independence or partition. <br/><br/>When a train full of corpses arriv</span>
  <span id="freeText2546118776720625553" style="display:none">The darkest period in Indian history coincided with the time India attained independence, because communal violence that broke out as a result of the partition of the country. Those on the wrong side of the Radcliffe line paid the price as rioters abducted, tortured, raped, killed and looted. Khushwant Singh's <i>Train to Pakistan</i> is a novel set in a fictional town Mano Majra where Muslims and Sikhs coexisted in peace, unaware of India's independence or partition. <br /><br />When a train full of corpses arrived in Mano Majra from Lahore, the air became heavy with fear, mistrust and vengeance and the situation in the town threatened to turn as tense as was the case in much of North India. Khushwant Singh's description of the gore and violence of partition is heart rending and mind numbing. A spark was all it took for people to give into mob mentality and as mentioned in the book, ethics - the kernel of religious code was conveniently removed from their thoughts and actions.<br /><br />Khushwant Singh has managed to capture the horrors of the partition of India, the entirety of which is insane and unfathomable especially if one considers the personal struggles of those caught in the meaningless warfare. Many of his descriptions etch harrowing images in our minds. But <i>Train to Pakistan</i> is a fine classic about love, sacrifice and crime in a period of religious and political madness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2546118776720625553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3436945164" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3436945164" />
    <a title="Tamoghna Biswas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/97513431-tamoghna-biswas"><img alt="Tamoghna Biswas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580746459p2/97513431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3436945164?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamoghna Biswas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamoghna Biswas" href="/user/show/97513431-tamoghna-biswas">Tamoghna Biswas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/53449492-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97513431-tamoghna-biswas?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97513431-tamoghna-biswas?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97513431-tamoghna-biswas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3436945164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3366636682544108370"><i><u><b>Pretext</b></u>: I don't feel the story deserves anything less than 5, but disturbing, or rather unsettling as it is, I can't just simply recommend it to everyone. And that's the only cons. I felt.</i><br/><br/>Before we were even introduced to the subject of history, we knew 1947 as the year India gained independence.<br/><br/>Later, much later we came to know that that was also the year of Partition.<br/><br/>Quite some time after that we came to know of these books (from elder ones), and our parents allowed us to read them even later</span>
  <span id="freeText3366636682544108370" style="display:none"><i><u><b>Pretext</b></u>: I don't feel the story deserves anything less than 5, but disturbing, or rather unsettling as it is, I can't just simply recommend it to everyone. And that's the only cons. I felt.</i><br /><br />Before we were even introduced to the subject of history, we knew 1947 as the year India gained independence.<br /><br />Later, much later we came to know that that was also the year of Partition.<br /><br />Quite some time after that we came to know of these books (from elder ones), and our parents allowed us to read them even later. Well, I did earlier though, my Dad believed in my choice. I, however don't know if I should have read it then, but if not then then now, for still I don't have a strong enough stomach to help me avoid sleepless nights after this. The time of Partition was<i> 'hard'</i> even if you <i>just </i>consider the interreligious riots. Hence, clearly enough the stories set on that time ought to be be just as chafed and cannibalistic as this one is, perhaps even more, for here you can't see the rawness...you rather feel it through the frightening ironical statements the author used, many times against even <i>our own</i> customs and superstitions.<br /><br />"<b>
  <i>Muslim said the Hindus had planned and started the killing. According to the Hindus, the Muslims were to blame. The fact is, both sides killed. Both shit and stabbed and speared and clubbed. Both tortured. Both raped."</i>
</b><br /><br />This was how the tale began.<br /><br />The distressing aspect is that the author, not for a single moment claimed entirety. He spoke in here<i> 'just'</i> about Mano Majra, and except the beginning, didn't talk about anyone,or anything irrelevant to it. Just to show you how it<i> 'was'</i> on the actual scale.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"You have seen the world and read many books, but take it from me that a snake can cast it's slough but not it's poison. ..."<br />..."There is no crime in anyone's blood any more than there is goodness in the blood of others,"...</i>
</b><br /><br />I will speak less about the story than even in the back cover. I only feel that books like these claim more the genre of realistic horror tales than historical fiction. Ruthless bandits, corrupt policemen, radical pseudo-intellectuals all are in here just like the people around you.Each and every character to whom the author gave reign one time or another generates a duality of disgust and sympathy in you. The best thing is that even the protagonist is of a villainous personality. So you see, you won't find any so-called ideologies in here from anyone. So, all in all, a very well-crafted story to stay awake a few nights. I did, and that's rare.<br /><b><br />"<i>'Come!</i>' they yelled. <i>'Come out, if you have the courage! Come out, if you want your mothers and sisters raped! Come out, brave men!</i>'<br />No one answered them. There was not a sound in Mano Majra."</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3366636682544108370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer3304743250030773503">The book is based during the summer of 1947 while the whole country was shaken with the woes of partition & the riots between the Sikhs and Muslims residing at Manoj Majra, a simple village where peace used to prevail amongst the said communities.<br/>The main protagonist, Juggut Singh, was notorious in the whole village as a gangster whose days fleeted between the jail, his humble home & the fields where in the name of work or laze used to secretly meet his love.<br/>*This relationship itself is a threat</span>
  <span id="freeText3304743250030773503" style="display:none">The book is based during the summer of 1947 while the whole country was shaken with the woes of partition &amp; the riots between the Sikhs and Muslims residing at Manoj Majra, a simple village where peace used to prevail amongst the said communities.<br />The main protagonist, Juggut Singh, was notorious in the whole village as a gangster whose days fleeted between the jail, his humble home &amp; the fields where in the name of work or laze used to secretly meet his love.<br />*This relationship itself is a threat to him<br />When the local moneylender got murdered one faithful night, he turned out to be the main suspect while there were valid reasons of why and why not he could be the main suspect<br />*His character is charismatically cool yet hateful<br />*He is the kind of character you would love to hate but you cannot ignore at all<br />It is the time of partition where Sikhs and Muslims were getting killed in the name of patriotism, blame games costing their lives; women getting raped and murdered; the young and old mercilessly killed in the name of the country being divided into India and Pakistan.<br />*Bodies were transferred to places where they got dumped &amp; burned<br />*There was never enough water to carry away the continuously dumped dead bodies; never enough fire to turn those bodies into ashes<br />*Arrangements were made, people made to leave homes; families were getting separated.<br />Who made the ultimate sacrifice to restore peace in such a situation?<br />✅Highlight of this story lies at the last page where the reader is hit with a jolt after a long journey of taking in all the cruelties, the deaths and separations, the corruptions and manipulations<br />👍What made the gangster stand out in the end made me really emotional<br />👎A laborious kind of read, violent scenes getting overly repeated, the slangs, too much graphic content in between the pages<br />Yet the ending &amp; the rawness of the writing style truimps in the end making me realise why it is a classic read.<br /><br />Recommended for age above 18.</span>

<div id="review_873335360" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/873335360" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/873335360?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143065883" href="/book/show/9500142-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=south-asian-fiction">south-asian-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=pakistan">pakistan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer873335360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14296272149581185651">One of the earliest English language novels to capture the horrors of the Indian Partition of 1947. Since then it has achieved classic status in the history of Subcontinental literature and for right reasons.<br/><br/>As independence and with it Partition took reality the Punjabis become desperate to learn about their fate. The province of Punjab (like Bengal on the Eastern borders) was to be cut in half between its Muslim majority population and Hindu-Sikh majority population. Those who found themselves </span>
  <span id="freeText14296272149581185651" style="display:none">One of the earliest English language novels to capture the horrors of the Indian Partition of 1947. Since then it has achieved classic status in the history of Subcontinental literature and for right reasons.<br /><br />As independence and with it Partition took reality the Punjabis become desperate to learn about their fate. The province of Punjab (like Bengal on the Eastern borders) was to be cut in half between its Muslim majority population and Hindu-Sikh majority population. Those who found themselves on the wrong side paid the price when in a village, where the story is set, neighbours and long terms friends turned against one another not knowing to which new country the village had gone, since it was on the border and had a fairly mixed population.<br /><br />The inanity and mindlessness of the conflict is cleverly depicted throughout the novel especially in a case when Iqbal, on the run, finds shelter in a mosque. His is a name that at that time passed for both Hindu and Muslim, with only the surname giving away his background. So originally <i>Iqbal Singh</i> became <i>Iqbal Khan</i> in the mosque.<br /><br />A trainload of corpses that came in from areas that were awarded to Pakistan triggers a chain reaction of riots in cities and villages where peace had hitherto prevailed. The story shows how one small incidence in a faraway town can start up a massive bloodshed in places where, despite tensions, people had lived peacefully before but now must shed one another's blood in the name of the new countries that existed only as arbitrary lines drawn by Imperial cartographers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14296272149581185651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_803801252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/803801252" />
    <a title="Ananya Chaudhuri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13653644-ananya-chaudhuri"><img alt="Ananya Chaudhuri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390501467p2/13653644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/803801252?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ananya Chaudhuri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ananya Chaudhuri" href="/user/show/13653644-ananya-chaudhuri">Ananya Chaudhuri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143417965" href="/book/show/17203304-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer803801252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7520035077768816558">Train to Pakistan is a book about the horrors of civil war and how a small peaceful village finally becomes a part of religious hate and communal violence. It depicts the myriads of human emotions which arise in troubled times and portrays how nothing is concrete. Khushwant Singh makes his point with this book that there is no categorical distinction of a person as good or bad and that even the best of relationships, which are built upon the virtues of comradeship and empathy, can be totally eng</span>
  <span id="freeText7520035077768816558" style="display:none">Train to Pakistan is a book about the horrors of civil war and how a small peaceful village finally becomes a part of religious hate and communal violence. It depicts the myriads of human emotions which arise in troubled times and portrays how nothing is concrete. Khushwant Singh makes his point with this book that there is no categorical distinction of a person as good or bad and that even the best of relationships, which are built upon the virtues of comradeship and empathy, can be totally engulfed in troubled times by selfishness, intolerance, hypocrisy and falsehood. It also portrays that the test of ones character is only in tough times- via the contrast of the two lead characters of the novel. Whilst one, who is projected throughout the book as a man with sharp but logical judgements and respected for his capability to revolutionize, fails to take action when he is presented with an opportunity, the other, who has been portrayed as a typical badmash (bad/criminal), trouble-maker and fails to conform to socially-acceptable standards, sacrifices for much greater causes such as love, loyalty and humanity...<br /><br /> Khushwant Singh is a brilliant raconteur and his choice of words and the way he describes each action and thought of a character is extremely lucid, deeming this book a brilliant read even from a literary point of view.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7520035077768816558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_190708133" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190708133" />
    <a title="Saimah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5897033-saimah"><img alt="Saimah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335806739p2/5897033.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/190708133?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saimah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saimah" href="/user/show/5897033-saimah">Saimah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190708133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3646174062177081389">Inspite of volumes being written about life before, during and after the Indo-Pak partition, 'Train to Pakistan' clearly stands apart. How the brotherhood between two major communities of a small peaceful village transforms to hatred and loathe overnight under the existing scenario, is unbelievably surprising. Murders, thefts, molestations, massacres, over just a short span of time are enough to send shivers down your spine.<br/><br/>The plot goes from being horrifying to disturbing, all the while keeping</span>
  <span id="freeText3646174062177081389" style="display:none">Inspite of volumes being written about life before, during and after the Indo-Pak partition, 'Train to Pakistan' clearly stands apart. How the brotherhood between two major communities of a small peaceful village transforms to hatred and loathe overnight under the existing scenario, is unbelievably surprising. Murders, thefts, molestations, massacres, over just a short span of time are enough to send shivers down your spine.<br /><br />The plot goes from being horrifying to disturbing, all the while keeping the readers' interest intact. The mentality of villagers, their opinion on freedom and independence, the law makers and law changers, have all been wonderfully described. The dilemmas and the questions raised by the author, be it in terms of religion, ethics or goodness make you pause and ponder over the issue. All the characters with their various strengths and weaknesses give the story a strong foundation. Add to it, the moral paradox has so beautifully been written that one can just hope for the book to go on and on.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3646174062177081389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2435629108" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2435629108" />
    <a title="Zaki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59706402-zaki"><img alt="Zaki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591019427p2/59706402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2435629108?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zaki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zaki" href="/user/show/59706402-zaki">Zaki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59706402-zaki?shelf=personal-collection">personal-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59706402-zaki?shelf=2k19-reads">2k19-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59706402-zaki?shelf=english-literature">english-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59706402-zaki?shelf=favourite">favourite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59706402-zaki?shelf=partition">partition</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2435629108" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4718373018556029243">This novel is both beautiful and heartbreaking. Not only the narration, also the way Khushwant Singh moved the story is impressive. In just 200 pages, he has covered almost all the cultural aspects of the partition. If you have to read one small but comprehensive book on the partition, 'Train to Pakistan' is for you. But if you want a lengthy but equally engrossing novel, you can go for 'This is not That Dawn'.<br/><br/>Khushwant Singh is a master of cultural history. In the novel, he gives the intricate </span>
  <span id="freeText4718373018556029243" style="display:none">This novel is both beautiful and heartbreaking. Not only the narration, also the way Khushwant Singh moved the story is impressive. In just 200 pages, he has covered almost all the cultural aspects of the partition. If you have to read one small but comprehensive book on the partition, 'Train to Pakistan' is for you. But if you want a lengthy but equally engrossing novel, you can go for 'This is not That Dawn'.<br /><br />Khushwant Singh is a master of cultural history. In the novel, he gives the intricate details about the Punjabi culture. The perspectives of people belonging to the different fields of life are given a gripping way.<br /><i>"Not many people said 'thank you' in these parts."<br />"The Punjabis love people they can pity." </i><br /><br />Independence meant differently for different people. For a political party, it meant to get political positions and to get more power. For educated ones, it meant to get more government jobs and to reach to the upper echelons of power. But, for common people, it was not less than a catastrophe. They remained in the battered condition in the same way as they were living in the British era. Even now, after 72 years, one third of population in India and Pakistan is living below the poverty line.<br /><i>"Freedom must be a good thing. But what will we get out of it? Educated people like you, Babu Sahib, will get the jobs the English had. Will we get more lands or more buffaloes?"<br />"Freedom is for the educated people who fought for it. We were slaves of the English, now we will be slaves of the educated Indians---or the Pakistanis." <br />"This new government is taking very loudly of stamping out all this. After a few months in office their enthusiasm will cool and things will go on as before."</i><br /><br />Not all of them migrated willingly as a 'sacrifice', as usually told in the nationalistic histories. They were forced to leave their ancestral homes. In the villages, where majority of the population was living, they were not even aware of the new countries being carved out, new lines being drawn. In some cases, only when military came to escort them out, they suddenly came to know about those events. <br /><i>"Quite suddenly, every Sikh in Mano Majra became a stranger with an evil intent."<br />For the first time, the name Pakistan came to mean something to them---a refuge where there were no Sikhs."<br />"No one had the nerve to suggest throwing them out even purely in a Sikh gathering." </i><br /><br />At places, he also talks about the colonial mindset presistent in our daily lives. <br /><i>"The memsahibs are like houris from paradise - white and soft like silk. All we have here are black buffaloes."<br />"Even the English respect a man of religion."</i><br /><br />The character of Iqbal is very interesting. He is a foreign-educated comrade. He wants to bring change here. But he looks at everything with contempt. He doesn't realize he can't change people without understanding them. He abhors even those things which ordinary people holds sacred. All Iqbal does is talking. Even at the end it is Jugga Badmash who saves people while the comrade keeps on sleeping. <br /><br />Hukam Chand is a deputy commissioner. In the area, his commands are followed by everyone. But, a person even with such authority could be hollow inside. <br /><i>"He did not want to sleep with the girl, or make love to her, or even to kiss her on the lips and feel her body. He simply wanted her to sleep her in his lap with her head resting on his chest." </i><br /><br />Manto writes in his stories that people were more worried about trains becoming unclean with blood than spilling blood. Same scene is depicted here, <br /><i>"Why should one who enjoyed killing human beings be bothered by a stray dog getting under his wheels." </i><br /><br />The most heart-wrecking scene was when Noora was leaving the village and went to see Jugga's mother. <br /><i>"Beybey, I am going away and will never come back again. Don't be harsh to me just when I am leaving." <br />"A vague hope filled Nooran's being. She felt as if she belonged to the house and the house to her; the charpoy she sat on, the buffalo, Jugga's mother, all were hers. She would come back even if Jugga failed to turn up."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4718373018556029243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327087-neha?shelf=saw-movie-version-too">saw-movie-version-too</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327087-neha?shelf=partition">partition</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer243295884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7069617307712003164">Probably one of the best works of fiction by Khushwant Singh and the Indo-Pak partition saga. Train to Pakistan is a classic story of human endurance and struggle through a mass movement. Freedom got us our own government, our own flag, our own anthem, our own country, but for a commoner he lost his own house, his own people, his own identity. The story is as simple and common as possible – a small peaceful village, regular corrupt officials, ordinary ruffians and holy men. The partition wave hi</span>
  <span id="freeText7069617307712003164" style="display:none">Probably one of the best works of fiction by Khushwant Singh and the Indo-Pak partition saga. Train to Pakistan is a classic story of human endurance and struggle through a mass movement. Freedom got us our own government, our own flag, our own anthem, our own country, but for a commoner he lost his own house, his own people, his own identity. The story is as simple and common as possible – a small peaceful village, regular corrupt officials, ordinary ruffians and holy men. The partition wave hits them like a hurricane and scatters them all over – some kill, some die, some run, some give up, some watch, some plot, some fight. Isn’t any tragedy just a multiplication of all our personal tragedies and any good deed just an individual motive. <br /><br />Khushwant Singh handles the subject sensitively and truthfully never loosing his control over reality. The government officials remain corrupt but with a damage control agenda, they twist right and wrong only to do what seems best. The local ruffians become the great saviours of religion and God, the avengers of the blood of brothers and honour of the women. The local men live their ordinary lives giving in to destiny and the orders from the high command. The narrative gets powerful and hair raising at the description of arriving train loads of mass dead bodies. But in all this one insignificant man, one ordinary thug, saves hundreds of lives, changing the course of history with only one simple act of love. A must read. <br /><br />Some interesting portions of the book:<br /><br />Population – How could you check increase in population? In the land of Kamasutra, the home of phallic worship and the son cult.<br /><br />We say Das (10) Number ka Badmash – derived from the number of the police register in which names of bad characters are listed.<br /><br />We say 420 to cheaters and liars – derived from Section 420 of the Indian Penal Code which defines the offence of cheating.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7069617307712003164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
