<div id="review_94543145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94543145" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938544-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330120106p2/1938544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94543145?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1938544-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">History teachers, students of the middle east, inquiring minds</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Brandon Hunziker
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94543145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14644342565964738396">This is one of the books we used to teach the class "The World After 1945" at the University of North Carolina. As a teaching tool for history, it is mixed, although it is a very interesting read. Its strengths are in introducing students to an environment most know little or nothing about (the northern border areas of India) and to ethnic and religious divisions very different from those in the US. It's major weakness is that, as a work of fiction, it is not representative of actual historical </span>
  <span id="freeText14644342565964738396" style="display:none">This is one of the books we used to teach the class "The World After 1945" at the University of North Carolina. As a teaching tool for history, it is mixed, although it is a very interesting read. Its strengths are in introducing students to an environment most know little or nothing about (the northern border areas of India) and to ethnic and religious divisions very different from those in the US. It's major weakness is that, as a work of fiction, it is not representative of actual historical fact, and may give more insight into the mind of the author than into social or political realities. <br /><br />The story centers around a town names Mano Majra, in which "Sikhs and Muslims have lived together in peace for thousands of years." The partition of the country into a Hindu state (India) and a Muslim one (Pakistan) at first barely affects this community, but soon refugees are traveling the lone daily train that stops in this remote village, and tensions begin to build. The breaking point comes when a train filled with corpses arrives and demonstrates to the residents the horrors of the civil war raging around them. Suddenly neighbor turns on neighbor, and paranoid plots are developed. People who had been close friends suddenly regard one another as vicious enemies, and (of course) a romeo and juliet subplot develops about a young Sikh in love with a young Muslim. While parts of the story seem contrived, it does present a very convincing view of a world divided by religious intolerance and fear. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14644342565964738396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3346815754" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3346815754" />
    <a title="Vartika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63883678-vartika"><img alt="Vartika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601807242p2/63883678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3346815754?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vartika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vartika" href="/user/show/63883678-vartika">Vartika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/53449492-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3346815754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10175081238944053315">The Partition is undoubtedly the deepest, darkest, bloodiest wound in the collective memory of South Asia, one that displaced over 14 million people, killed 2 million, and suspended a hithertho harmonious subcontinent into mutual suspicion and hatred that permeates till this day. <br/><br/>I was 11 when I first read this book — too young, and bred too far from the horrors it speaks of, to fully understand. Yet, like every child on either side of the Radcliffe Line, I had grown up — and continued to grow u</span>
  <span id="freeText10175081238944053315" style="display:none">The Partition is undoubtedly the deepest, darkest, bloodiest wound in the collective memory of South Asia, one that displaced over 14 million people, killed 2 million, and suspended a hithertho harmonious subcontinent into mutual suspicion and hatred that permeates till this day. <br /><br />I was 11 when I first read this book — too young, and bred too far from the horrors it speaks of, to fully understand. Yet, like every child on either side of the Radcliffe Line, I had grown up — and continued to grow up — conscious and mournful of the Partition. Today, with the air full of communal hate and vitriol — and violence — yet again, I felt compelled to pick this up anew. <br /><br />Khushwant Singh's <i> Train To Pakistan </i> is perhaps the best known work about the Partition, and turned out to be a poignant, insightful reminder of what we lost — and why. The story is set in the fictional village of Mano Majra, located near a railway line next to the Sutlej on the Punjab border. The village is not only a microcosm of pre-Independence communal harmony, interdependence and friendship — a morality based on loyalty to fellow villagers — but also of the general indifference and unawareness in much of the country about the politics of Independence and the Partition. All of that is to change when a train full of corpses arrives from the other side. At the heart of it, this book explores the unbelievability of the violence of 1947: How did distrust so easily poison lifelong trust and camaraderie? How were people so driven by blood and vengeance to wipe out their own neighbours? And who is to blame?<br /><br /><i> Train to Pakistan </i> presents its answers in a subtle critique of power, as well as of morality. Throughout the novel, Singh constructs parallels between its male characters. The first of these is established by the Gunshot sequence: one night, the moneylender of the village is murdered. As the gunshot resounds, the two men in question — Jugga, a dacoit, and Hukum Chand, the divisional magistrate — tear away from the women they have been engaged with (more precisely, in the act of rape), both of whom are 16 years old. While in Jugga's case, the girl is his girlfriend Nooran, the daughter of a Muslim weaver; Hukum Chand is involved with a prostitute who, as he himself muses to himself rather guiltily, is the same age as his own daughter would have been. Is one better than the other? While the latter is a figure of authority, he remains fatalistic and amoral throughout the novel. In fact, his deliberate misdirections spark the growth of communal distrust between the people of Mano Majra. Meanwhile, Jugga lays down his life trying to prevent the rioters in the village from attacking a train full of Muslims at the end of the book. <br /><br />Another parallel is drawn between Jugga and the foreign-educated, city-bred social worker Iqbal, who are arrested and detained together. Iqbal comes to Mano Majra to educated the peasants against the exploitative capitalist class and state. He is prone to sermonising and theoretically perceptive (such as to the problem with prisons). However, despite his understanding of class and caste divisions, he does not actively oppose the privileged treatment he recieves from Jugga in custody; he does not try to mix with the villagers, is a city boy distrustful of their food and water, and maintains himself as an outsider. While a privileged character who philosophises and talks about many larger issues, such as true Freedom, he is detached from the realities faced by most people in the country. In fact, it is Jugga who bring out the more nuanced ideas in this novel, such as the colonial perception of education: he is representative of an entire mindset when he thinks of English as real education, while Urdu and Gurmukhi being mere signs of literacy. <br /><br />However, Iqbal's character is important in bringing out the author's critique of the newly-formed government in the novel. He voices the farce that the transfer of power from the British to Indians signified, remarking that freedom for the rich means the ability to rule and exercise power themselves, while for the poor it still is slavery. Throughout the book, Singh talks about the divide between the "<i> baniyas </i>" or upperclass-and-caste men in the parliament celebrating Independence and giving speeches about the country's 'tryst with destiny' and the people dying on the border. This is hardly a surprising critique coming from a Sikh man who survived the Partition violence, but it still was not as full-bodied a critique as it could be — hardly surprising, either, as Singh <i> was </i> a sycophant to later Congress governments and a supporter of the National Emergency of 1975. <br /><br />Yet, <i> Train to Pakistan </i> on the whole does see authority as a cause and enabler of the massacres — the police officers, both those under Hukum Chand and those who accompany the refugee convoys later, are seen as complicit, whether indirectly or otherwise. Surprisingly, Singh, known also for his sexism, even points fingers at the easily-threatened concepts of masculinity that pervade the Indian consciousness: when rioters enter Mano Majra, they taunt the villagers as "eunuchs" for not rising up to avenge Sikh blood, and incite them to act as a "Martial race". Again, in Singh's case it is, of course, best to see this attack exclusively in the context it's provided in. <br /><br />To his credit, Khushwant Singh does emphasise that bloodshed and terror came from both sides and both communities, without necessarily trying to equate or measure them against each other: while much of the action in <i> Train to Pakistan </i> takes place on the Indian side and is therefore threatening to Muslims, a drunk Hukum Chand towards the end is seen remembering the horrors seen by Sikhs and Hindus on the other side. He also points at the lawlessness of the age, beginning the novel with a dacoity, and also showing how refugee properties were often purposedly given to plunderers. Another mark of the time, ofcourse, can be found in Khushwant Singh's writing itself: in the fashion of all Indians writing in the English language in this period, he takes a confused stance on his usage of words from Indian languages, often replacing them with anglicised or English translations. This is often awkward — <i> sarkaar, </i> which implies lordship or an authority figure, becomes "government."<br /><br />In the end, however, <i> Train to Pakistan </i> does what it sets out to amazingly well, which is to retain the realities of the conflict that has shaped millions of lives over 7 decades now in collective and literary memory with honesty, understanding, and suitable pathos.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10175081238944053315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_3346815754'><a id="like_count_review_3346815754" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/3346815754?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">12 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F785454.Train_to_Pakistan">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_1552240336" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1552240336" />
    <a title="Asha Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5040094-asha-seth"><img alt="Asha Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605471653p2/5040094.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1552240336?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Asha Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Asha Seth" href="/user/show/5040094-asha-seth">Asha Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143065883" href="/book/show/9500142-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5040094-asha-seth?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5040094-asha-seth?shelf=indian-lit">indian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1552240336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15330065418636921443"><br/><br/>When I finished reading Train to Pakistan, I asked myself what was I thinking while I planned on reading this one. It surely is not what meets the eye at mere synopsis glance. It also isn’t what one would think of – a post-war scenario. No. It is a clear picture. One can see the country being torn apart, shredding humanity to pieces, leaving behind hearts that would bleed for years to come. First published in 1956, the book bore the horrendous memories of the holocaust that were still fresh in e</span>
  <span id="freeText15330065418636921443" style="display:none"><br /><br />When I finished reading Train to Pakistan, I asked myself what was I thinking while I planned on reading this one. It surely is not what meets the eye at mere synopsis glance. It also isn’t what one would think of – a post-war scenario. No. It is a clear picture. One can see the country being torn apart, shredding humanity to pieces, leaving behind hearts that would bleed for years to come. First published in 1956, the book bore the horrendous memories of the holocaust that were still fresh in everybody’s minds.<br /><br />Mano Majra is a village in the post-partition India. Muslims and Sikhs dwell unaffected by the horrific outcome of partition. They live in peace. But surrounded by mobs thirsty to rob, kill, torture and rape. It begins with the murder of the local money lender Ram Lal who was killed by a gang led by dacoit Malli but Juggut Singh, a Sikh man with bad reputation is arrested as suspect.<br /><br />Iqbal is an educated social reformer interested in politics has just arrived in the village and is also arrested for the same murder.<br /><br />Hukum Chand is a powerful yet corrupt authority and had ordered arrests of Juggut and Iqbal. He is fighting a battle within himself to get rid of his guilt an outcome of his unethical and evil treatment of the Muslims and Hindus.<br /><br />As the story unfolds, we can feel tension creeping into the village. People are being misled. Rumors that would chill their nerves are making way into their hearts. People huddle in their houses after sunset. Innocent people are being put behind bars for crimes they never committed. The criminals are at loose, attacking families in villages, robbing them, murdering them.<br /><br />The most horrific part yet are the trains that arrive at night loaded with corpses of thousands of people sent from Pakistan with messages that read – Gift to India from Pakistan. And each time, one of these trains arrives a depressing chill grips the village.<br /><br />And finally one day, their worst fear comes true when it is ordered that all the Muslims in Mano Majra would be sent to Pakistan. They are forced to leave the village, their home. None is aware of the murderous plot that is about to take place – all Muslims are to be murdered on the train and sent to Pakistan the same way train loads of Hindus were received in India.<br /><br />Iqbal, Hukum Chand and Juggut Singh are all aware of the mass murder planned and yet it is Juggut Singh who takes a step to foul the plan of murdering Muslims on the train. On the rainy night the attack is supposed to happen, he gets in the way to save thousands of lives and gets killed in the process.<br /><br />This novel gives vivid accounts of the massacres of Hindus, Muslims and Sikhs. It will paint a picture that will never fade from your memories. It is fiction but more of a peek into reality at the time. It is depressing, dark and dirty and yet when you are done reading you can only feel sorry for the families who were a part of the atrocities stemming from the partition; one of the bloodiest in the history of mankind.<br /><br />A must read, whether you are an Indian or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15330065418636921443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_737660325" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/737660325" />
    <a title="Sumit Singla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18177815-sumit-singla"><img alt="Sumit Singla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503976056p2/18177815.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/737660325?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumit Singla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumit Singla" href="/user/show/18177815-sumit-singla">Sumit Singla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18177815-sumit-singla?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18177815-sumit-singla?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18177815-sumit-singla?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer737660325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10392179787785894335">The Partition of 1947 has probably been one of the most horrific and traumatic events that Modern India has faced. Belonging to the State of Punjab, which suffered the most due to Partition, I've heard the bloodcurdling tales of that time from elders in the family.<br/><br/>But, none of them as gory or visceral as Khushwant Singh's narrative about it.<br/><br/>The book had been on my TBRs for a while, but what made me pick it up immediately was a train to very close to Pakistan - Amritsar. Lahore, one of the most i</span>
  <span id="freeText10392179787785894335" style="display:none">The Partition of 1947 has probably been one of the most horrific and traumatic events that Modern India has faced. Belonging to the State of Punjab, which suffered the most due to Partition, I've heard the bloodcurdling tales of that time from elders in the family.<br /><br />But, none of them as gory or visceral as Khushwant Singh's narrative about it.<br /><br />The book had been on my TBRs for a while, but what made me pick it up immediately was a train to very close to Pakistan - Amritsar. Lahore, one of the most important cities of pre-independence Punjab was only about 20 km away from where I was sitting and watching the beating retreat ceremony.<br /><br />Through the writer's eyes, I can almost see the trains overflowing with refugees pulling into stations on either side of the border and the carnage accompanying them.<br /><br />Singh's fictional Mano Majra has been spared most of the horrors of Partition and Sikhs, Muslims, and Hindus live in peace with each other. However, the coming of the 'ghost train' from Pakistan, loaded with corpses of thousands changes all that. The secular fabric of the village lies shattered among the suspicion and hatred that is fostered among its residents. <br /><br />What makes this book different and extremely powerful are the imperfections of the characters themselves. There's Hukum Chand, the indecisive magistrate, trying to bed a girl young enough to be his daughter. Meet Singh, the 'bhai' of the gurudwara, who tries but fails to talk sense into brains addled by communal hatred. Juggut 'Jugga' Singh, the ex-dacoit and ostensible murderer, with a pivotal role and probably the best understanding of honour. Iqbal Singh, who sounds like a 'comrade', with his bright ideas of equality and social justice.<br /><br />Khushwant Singh uses occasional sardonic wit and his inimitable style to narrate a story that reaches a shrieking crescendo much like the noise of the train that the story revolves around.<br /><br />What makes me terribly sad is that though this book was written in 1956, even now, nearly 60 years later, India hasn't moved on from communal tension and hatred. Watching the ceremony at the border, I realized how we're conditioned to celebrate the divide and be proud of how different/evolved/developed we are, as compared to our neighbours.<br /><br />However, I don't think we try to understand and realize that the people across the border are essentially the same, with minor variations in the language they speak or the god they worship. If only, more people understood and heeded the message in Singh's book...<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>India is constipated with a lot of humbug. Take religion. For the Hindu, it means little besides caste and cow-protection. For the Muslim, circumcision and kosher meat. For the Sikh, long hair and hatred of the Muslim. For the Christian, Hinduism with a sola topee. For the Parsi, fire-worship and feeding vultures. Ethics, which should be the kernel of a religious code, has been carefully removed. </blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10392179787785894335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2220020109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13260934647746716425">Train to Pakistan is simple yet gripping and thought-provoking novel. Even though it is a fictional work, I feel there can be no doubt of stories like this to be a reality. The story is at the time of the partition of India and Pakistan but the book is far from throwing light on political disturbances, instead, it's about social understanding among the people living in a particular place. One of the three main characters, Hukum Chand, an alcoholic is drowned under the guilt of not being useful. </span>
  <span id="freeText13260934647746716425" style="display:none">Train to Pakistan is simple yet gripping and thought-provoking novel. Even though it is a fictional work, I feel there can be no doubt of stories like this to be a reality. The story is at the time of the partition of India and Pakistan but the book is far from throwing light on political disturbances, instead, it's about social understanding among the people living in a particular place. One of the three main characters, Hukum Chand, an alcoholic is drowned under the guilt of not being useful. Iqbal Singh, an educated social worker kept on dreaming as to what should be done while Juggut Singh not knowing how to read and write, did what he felt was right. He saved the train filled with Muslims passengers; one of the passengers being his lover Nooran, by sacrificing his own life. <br />Killing each other and cutting into pieces, abusing females, abducting kids. The story is such that no side can be blamed more for what's been done. The reason could be lack of education or lack of love.<br />All I can say is a quote from a famous movie, it doesn't matter what you are underneath, what you do that defines you.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1730907821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1730907821" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2528841-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336501182p2/2528841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1730907821?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/2528841-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19249226-train-to-pakistan">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Claire by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/10522355-maan">Maan</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=south-asian-literature">south-asian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=indian-literature">indian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=pakistan-literature">pakistan-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1730907821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17818791071813993587">There are so many tragic stories surrounding partition, thousands that will never be told because there is no one left to tell them. It was a moment in history that showed what happens to humanity when fear and panic really take hold and its devastating.<br/><br/>I'm glad this wasn't written this century, when books and films are so much more visceral and confrontational with their message, written in 1956, .<br/><br/>Khushwant Singh takes the Punjabi farming village of Mano Majra, a small village on the border bet</span>
  <span id="freeText17818791071813993587" style="display:none">There are so many tragic stories surrounding partition, thousands that will never be told because there is no one left to tell them. It was a moment in history that showed what happens to humanity when fear and panic really take hold and its devastating.<br /><br />I'm glad this wasn't written this century, when books and films are so much more visceral and confrontational with their message, written in 1956, .<br /><br />Khushwant Singh takes the Punjabi farming village of Mano Majra, a small village on the border between India and Pakistan, one of strategic importance to the railway system, and narrates the moment when news of this change arrives and shows how it affects this community. <br /><br />The trains are symbolic to the story, they are the portenders of what is to come, in the past they have run like clockwork and though they rarely stop, the lives of the villages is intricately linked to their passing. When the trains become delayed, the rhythm of the village gets out of sync and worse when the trains begin to stop in Mano Majra, life will never be the same for all who witness it.<br /><br />A man named Iqbal arrives from Delhi, sent by his party to observe the impact of the news.<br /><blockquote>Well, Babuji,' began the Muslim. 'Tell us something. What is happening in the world?<br /> What is all this about about Pakistan and Hindustan?<br />'We live in this little village and know nothing,' the lambardar put in. 'Babuji, tell us, why did the English leave?'</blockquote><br />The villagers aren't convinced by Iqbal's idealistic view that freedom will follow Independence.<br /><blockquote>'Why, don't you people want to be free? Do you want to remain slaves all your lives?<br />After a long silence the lambardar(Headman/Revenue collector) answered: 'Freedom must be a good thing. But what will we get out of it? Educated people like you, Babu Sahib, will get the jobs the English had. Will we get more land or more buffaloes?<br />'No,' the Muslim said. 'Freedom is for the educated people who fought for it. We were slaves of the English, now we will be slaves of the educated Indians - or the Pakistanis.'</blockquote><br />Brilliant, a compelling read, and one that was relatively respectful to all who were affected and to readers with vivid imaginations, who don't need certain scenes described in overly graphic detail.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17818791071813993587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1723718893" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1723718893" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1723718893?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=5-indian">5-indian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1723718893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12369263451455086505"><blockquote>
  <i>“ Not forever does the bulbul sing<br/> In balmy shades of bowers,<br/> Not forever lasts the spring<br/> Nor ever blossom flowers.<br/> Not forever reigneth joy,<br/> Sets the sun on days of bliss,<br/> Friendships not forever last,<br/> They know not life, who know not this.”</i>
  <br/>
</blockquote><br/>Khushwant Singh was one of the most popular authors in India. Serious literary as well as light humorous fiction, journalism etc he was everywhere. And even if you are not a reader, you need to love him for him for his humor, he actually came up with</span>
  <span id="freeText12369263451455086505" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“ Not forever does the bulbul sing<br /> In balmy shades of bowers,<br /> Not forever lasts the spring<br /> Nor ever blossom flowers.<br /> Not forever reigneth joy,<br /> Sets the sun on days of bliss,<br /> Friendships not forever last,<br /> They know not life, who know not this.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Khushwant Singh was one of the most popular authors in India. Serious literary as well as light humorous fiction, journalism etc he was everywhere. And even if you are not a reader, you need to love him for him for his humor, he actually came up with his brand of jokes – the Santa-Banta jokes.<br />*<br />‘Train to Pakistan’ is the best novel about the subcontinent that I’ve read this far. It is about religious riots in and around Punjab at the time of independence of the country in which an estimated 14 million people were migrated over something as fictional as God. 2 million were killed.<br /><br />You know how Dawkins talks about a world without religion? well there you have it, 2 million lesser deaths which includes children and raped women. All done in sweeet name of God<br /><br />The few trains from either side that would cross the border were always overcrowded – Singh estimates a typical train with about a thousand people in it and another four-five hundred on roof. The train would be stopped mid-way by rioters, and by the time it crossed the border – it would be full of death bodies.<br /><br />You know another thing about a world without religion – lesser political boundaries. Nations are another piece of fiction we could do without. It is the childish for people to fight for something (their nation) for a century and then as soon as you get it – divide it into pieces.<br /><br />If you think ‘childish’ is not the right word, think of last time you judged between two children who just had a fight. Was their response ‘he/she did it first’? Well that is exactly how those involved in those riots justified it. <br /><br />And these are the people who were living in harmony for centuries! <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>‘Who is it?’ asked the lambardar, shading his eyes from the lamp. ‘Come in.’<br /> Imam Baksh came in. Two others followed him. They also were Muslims.<br />‘Salaam, Chacha Imam Baksh. Salaam, Khair Dina. Salaam, salaam.’<br /> ‘Sat Sri Akal, Lambardara. Sat Sri Akal,’ answered the Muslims”<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />People who wouldn’t be capable of violence otherwise. Singh brings out the effects of mob violence really well. He is very secular in his writing style; holding equal disgust for all:<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />“Logic was never a strong point with Sikhs; when they were roused, logic did not matter at all.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />… and this is coming from a Sikh.<br /><br />And almost anyone can haunt you with descriptions of a train from Pakistan full of dead bodies, but it takes a Khushwant Singh to back it up with lines that make it imposible to paint all muslims in black:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“The northern horizon, which had turned a bluish grey, showed orange again. The orange turned into copper and then into a luminous russet. Red tongues of flame leaped into the black sky. A soft breeze began to blow towards the village. It brought the smell of burning kerosene, then of wood. And then—a faint acrid smell of searing flesh.<br /> The village was stilled in a deathly silence. No one asked anyone else what the odour was. They all knew. They had known it all the time. The answer was implicit in the fact that the train had come from Pakistan.<br /> That evening, for the first time in the memory of Mano Majra, Imam Baksh’s sonorous cry did not rise to the heavens to proclaim the glory of God.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />*<br /><b>
  <u>Part II</u>
</b><br />Mostly more ramblings against religions with a supid satire at the end. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />And of course, you could hear someone say that it is not about religion. That a true Hindu, Sikh or Muslim would never have do it – well, the people involved in religious violence always carry the right kind of dressing and speak right kind of slogans. They have always proclaimed themselves belonging to that religion all their lives. No one clarified until it was too late. No one throws them out of their respected temples or mosques before they commit violence. And if they were not caught, they would have returned to same religious places and still no one would have clarified:<br /><blockquote><br /><i>“they know what they are doing. They will kill. If it is a success, they will come to the gurdwara for thanksgiving. They will also make offerings to wash away their sins.”</i><br /></blockquote><br />But if they got caught, you want to tell the world they didn’t belong to your religion. Okay, throw them out, your choice! But you can’t do that with retrospective effect, you have to accept responsibility for what they already have done. <br /><br />Another thing you hear a lot is that they wouldn’t have done it if they had read their scriptures. That doesn’t make them any less religious. Does it? No religion I know of, has ever put any kind of entrance exam requiring knowledge of scriptures. In fact, every major religious community has a lot of illiterate population int it which is never said to be less religious on that account.<br /><br />And you know what kind of slogans did ylthose rioters used while killing each other. Slogan for group one Allahu Akbar, group two: Sat Shri Akal, group three: Har Har Mahadev. I mean do they even know what these slogans really mean? They mean ‘God is Greatest’, ‘God is ultimate truth’ and ‘May God take away our sorrows’. Potato Potato. They are supposed to be at same team. It is atheists they need to be looking for. Devil’s greatest achievement might have been convincing people that he is God.<br /><br />And now while I’m rambling, I was wondering how these rioters would have ask for forgiveness of their crimes. What reaction did they expect their God to have:<br /><br />Imagine a typical Sikh or Hindu rioter goes to temple asking for forgiveness.<br />“O Lord!” he prays “Forgive me for I’ve killed seven people today.”<br />Suddenly the God shows up and asks him “WHAT? Why would you do it?”<br />“They said it was their Allah that created the world, not you”<br />“Oh! Did they?” God looks away too embarrassed to tell him that was name he had taken while talking with Mohammad, “All right, you are forgiven.”<br />But our rioter doesn’t leave, “Ahm sir!”<br />“yes”<br />“I forget to tell you that three of them were children.”<br />“WHAT?”<br />“But ... but ... but” our rioter quickly adds in self defense “I did shouted aloud our religious slogan before doing so.”<br />“Then it is okay.” Says our all forgiving God.<br />But rioter still doesn’t leave. “Ahm sir!”<br />“Now what?”<br />“I forget this another little thing...”<br />“Be out with it” said God now a little furious.<br />“You see two of them were women ... and I might have raped them in passing.”<br />“WHAT! And did you enjoy it?”<br />“No sir!” says rioter pretending to be offended, “Not a bit of it. I had all my senses in control. It was all in your name.”<br />“Then it is okay. Now leave. I’ve an appointment somewhere else.”<br />Our rioter nods, salutes and leaves. As soon as he left, God too flew away, to across the borders where a Muslim rioter was waiting for him with request for a seat in heaven with all its full package of four virgins as a reward for similar acts he did against Hindu idol-worshippers and Sikh infidels in Almighty’s service. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />*<br /><b>
  <u>Part III</u>
</b><br />Not so serious quotes from book describing nature Indian society. Read if you don’t want to read book but want to know about Indians atititude towards marriage, education, sex etc. Not much have changed in over five-and-a-half decades since it was written.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <br />
  <u>Education</u>
  <br />
  <i><br />“For him, education meant knowing English. Clerks and letter writers who wrote Urdu or Gurmukhi were literate, but not educated.”<br /><u>Inequality</u><br /><i><br />“In a country which had accepted caste distinctions for many centuries, inequality had become an inborn mental concept. If caste was abolished by legislation, it came up in other forms of class distinction. In thoroughly westernized circles like that of the civil servants in the government secretariat in Delhi, places for parking cars were marked according to seniority, and certain entrances to offices were reserved for higher officials. Lavatories were graded according to rank and labelled SENIOR OFFICERS, JUNIOR OFFICERS, CLERKS AND STENOGRAPHERS and OTHER RANKS. “<br /></i><br /><u><br />Sex</u><br /><i><br />“It was not possible to keep Indians off the subject of sex for long. It obsessed their minds. It came out in their art, literature and religion. One saw it on the hoardings in the cities advertising aphrodisiacs and curatives for ill effects of masturbation. One saw it in the law courts and marketplaces, where hawkers did a thriving trade selling oil made of the skin of sand lizards to put life into tired groins and increase the size of the phallus. One read it in the advertisements of quacks who claimed to possess remedies for barrenness and medicines to induce wombs to yield male children. One heard about it all the time. No people used incestuous abuse quite as casually as did the Indians. Terms like sala, wife’s brother (‘I would like to sleep with your sister’), and susra, father-in-law (‘I would like to sleep with your daughter’) were as often terms of affection for one’s friends and relatives as expressions of anger to insult one’s enemies. Conversation on any topic—politics, philosophy, sport—soon came down to sex, which everyone enjoyed with a lot of giggling and hand-slapping.”<br /></i><br /><u>Matimonials</u><br /><i><br />“In disgust, he turned to the matrimonial ads. There was sometimes entertainment there. But the youth of the Punjab were as alike as the news. The qualities they required in a wife were identical. All wanted virgins. A few, more broad-minded than the rest, were willing to consider widows, but only if they had not been deflowered. All demanded women who were good at h. h. a., or household affairs. To the advanced and charitable, c. &amp; d. [caste and dowry] were no bar. Not many asked for photographs of their prospective wives. Beauty, they recognized, was only skin-deep. Most wanted to ‘correspond with horoscopes’. Astronomical harmony was the one guarantee of happiness. Iqbal threw the papers away, and rummaged through the magazines.”</i> <br /><u> <br />Religion</u><br /><i><br />“India is constipated with a lot of humbug. Take religion. For the Hindu, it means little besides caste and cow-protection. For the Muslim, circumcision and kosher meat. For the Sikh, long hair and hatred of the Muslim. For the Christian, Hinduism with a sola topee. For the Parsi, fire-worship and feeding vultures. Ethics, which should be the kernel of a religious code, has been carefully removed. Take philosophy, about which there is so much hoo-ha. It is just muddle-headedness masquerading as mysticism. And Yoga, particularly Yoga, that excellent earner of dollars! Stand on your head. Sit cross-legged and tickle your navel with your nose. Have perfect control over the senses. Make women come till they cry ‘Enough!’ and you can say ‘Next, please’ without opening your eyes. And all the mumbo-jumbo of reincarnation. Man into ox into ape into beetle into eight million four hundred thousand kinds of animate things. Proof? We do not go in for such pedestrian pastimes as proof! That is Western. We are of the mysterious East. No proof, just faith. No reason, just faith. Thought, which should be the sine qua non of a philosophical code, is dispensed with. We climb to sublime heights on the wings of fancy. We do the rope trick in all spheres of creative life. As long as the world credulously believes in our capacity to make a rope rise skyward and a little boy climb it till he is out of view, so long will our brand of humbug thrive.“<br /></i></i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="12369263451455086505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3497010322" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3497010322" />
    <a title="Kabir Kaur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/112236427-kabir-kaur"><img alt="Kabir Kaur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597495021p2/112236427.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3497010322?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kabir Kaur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kabir Kaur" href="/user/show/112236427-kabir-kaur">Kabir Kaur</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3497010322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7879747804269589905"> <br/>The book is a classic and requires little introduction. Khushwant Singh’s writing is a delight and this one’s the first book I ever read from the writer. Although I had read some of his works in my school textbooks, never had I read any of his books. <br/>This book is about the partition of Pakistan from India. There is no doubt that the event is the deadliest and the most gruesome chapter in chronicles of India and the topic is a personal one to me. I have grown up listening to stories of trauma an</span>
  <span id="freeText7879747804269589905" style="display:none"> <br />The book is a classic and requires little introduction. Khushwant Singh’s writing is a delight and this one’s the first book I ever read from the writer. Although I had read some of his works in my school textbooks, never had I read any of his books. <br />This book is about the partition of Pakistan from India. There is no doubt that the event is the deadliest and the most gruesome chapter in chronicles of India and the topic is a personal one to me. I have grown up listening to stories of trauma and agony from my grandmother's tongue, of losing family and struggling to keep alive. The book was a triggering one for me personally and the detailed description of barbarity engulfed me with dejection. <br />The story based in a small village, paints a vivid picture of traditional Punjab. It features a set of god fearing people who are full of innocence and indifference, leading their lives with a handful of prejudice that falls easily at the hands of manipulators. <br />A series of unusual departures and arrivals of special trains at the Railway Station causes unrest in these lives and digresses the peace in the village to affliction. The story portrays the political premises and inclinations of the time, the attempts to creating a proletariat revolution, the appreciation and faith in Gandhi to his criticism, and weaponising religion for power and politics. <br />Not much has changed since the time, comrades are still trying to erect a revolution, Gandhi and Nehru are still being appreciated by some and blamed by others, religious intolerance and mass murders have not become a thing of the past yet but, there are fewer dacoits and" badmash" on the streets walking with spears and guns than the ones in power, the ruling class, sitting in their safari suits. The book reinstated my belief in the essential goodness of human character, and delineated how the fear of oblivion after sacrificing one’s life overpowers the ability to reason and to process all the education and ideals procured through its lifetime. <br />It took me some time to delve into the reading, a few English translations of commonly used Hindi-Punjabi words put me off and I had to push myself to read ahead and not form an immediate opinion and I am really glad that I did read. The story keeps growing one sentence at a time quite neatly. What I loved the most about the writing was how it was inclusive but at the same time not very long or tiring to follow. It amazed me how freedom meant little to simple people like these village dwellers and how little were they affected by the turn of tables in India’s political hegemony. Singh’s description of events was so lucid that I could literally sketch each scene in my head. The bittersweet ending of the story made me sigh as I wondered about the thought processes of the characters and how their actions brought the story to a finish. <br />The book is for everyone and I personally feel that it is one of those classics that we all must read at some point in our lives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7879747804269589905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Nar<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15414468982007977079\" style=\"display:none\">A reimagining of the world-famous Indian epic, the Mahabharat—told from the point of view of an amazing woman.Relevant to today’s war-torn world, The Palace of Illusions takes us back to a time that is half history, half myth, and wholly magical. Narrated by Panchaali, the wife of the legendary Pandavas brothers in the Mahabharat, the novel gives us a new interpretation of this ancient tale. The novel traces the princess Panchaali\'s life, beginning with her birth in fire and following her spirited balancing act as a woman with five husbands who have been cheated out of their father’s kingdom. Panchaali is swept into their quest to reclaim their birthright, remaining at their side through years of exile and a terrible civil war involving all the important kings of India. Meanwhile, we never lose sight of her strategic duels with her mother-in-law, her complicated friendship with the enigmatic Krishna, or her secret attraction to the mysterious man who is her husbands\' most dangerous enemy. Panchaali is a fiery female redefining for us a world of warriors, gods, and the ever-manipulating hands of fate.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15414468982007977079\" href=\"#\" Servant. Philosopher. Entrepreneur. Murderer. Over the course of seven nights, by the scattered light of a preposterous chandelier, Balram tells us the terrible and transfixing story of how he came to be a success in life—having nothing but his own wits to help him along. \n\nBorn in the dark heart of India, Balram gets a break when he is hired as a driver for his village\'s wealthiest man, two house Pomeranians (Puddles and Cuddles), and the rich man\'s (very unlucky) son. From behind the wheel of their Honda City car, Balram\'s new world is a revelation. While his peers flip through the pages of Murder Weekly (&quot;Love -- Rape -- Revenge!&quot;), barter for girls, drink liquor (Thunderbolt), and perpetuate the Great Rooster Coop of Indian society, Balram watches his employers bribe foreign ministers for tax breaks, barter for girls, drink liquor (single-malt whiskey), and play their own role in the Rooster Coop. Balram learns how to siphon gas, deal with corrupt mechanics, and refill and resell Johnnie Walker Black Label bottles (all but one). He also finds a way out of the Coop that no one else inside it can perceive. \n\nBalram\'s eyes penetrate India as few outsiders can: the cockroaches and the call centers; the prostitutes and the worshippers; the ancient and Internet cultures; the water buffalo and, trapped in so many kinds of cages that escape is (almost) impossible, the white tiger. And with a charisma as undeniable as it is unexpected, Balram teaches us that religion doesn\'t create virtue, and money doesn\'t solve every problem -- but decency can still be found in a corrupt world, and you can get what you want out of life if you eavesdrop on the right conversations. \n\nThe White Tiger recalls The Death of Vishnu and Bangkok 8 in ambition, scope, and narrative genius, with a mischief and personality all its own. The heartbreaking story of Kashmir has so far been told through the prism of the brutality of the Indian state, and the pro-independence demands of separatists. But there is another part of the story that has remained unrecorded and buried. Our Moon Has Blood Clots is the unspoken chapter in the story of Kashmir, in which it was purged of the Kashmiri Pandit community in a violent ethnic cleansing backed by Islamist militants. Hundreds of people were tortured and killed, and about 3,50,000 Kashmiri Pandits were forced to leave their homes and spend the rest of their lives in exile in their own country. Rahul Pandita has written a deeply personal, powerful and unforgettable story of history, home and loss.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17664110712798026237\" href=\"#\" <span id="freeTextContainer17862490144808929959">Khushwant Singh, (Punjabi: ਖ਼ੁਸ਼ਵੰਤ ਸਿੰਘ, Hindi: खुशवंत सिंह) born on 2 February 1915 in Hadali, Undivided India, (now a part of Pakistan), was a prominent Indian novelist and journalist. Singh's weekly column, "With Malice towards One and All", carried by several Indian newspapers, was among the most widely-read columns in the country.<br/><br/>An important post-colonial novelist writing in English, Singh </span>
  <span id="freeText17862490144808929959" style="display:none">Khushwant Singh, (Punjabi: ਖ਼ੁਸ਼ਵੰਤ ਸਿੰਘ, Hindi: खुशवंत सिंह) born on 2 February 1915 in Hadali, Undivided India, (now a part of Pakistan), was a prominent Indian novelist and journalist. Singh's weekly column, "With Malice towards One and All", carried by several Indian newspapers, was among the most widely-read columns in the country.<br /><br />An important post-colonial novelist writing in English, Singh is best known for his trenchant secularism, his humor, and an abiding love of poetry. His comparisons of social and behavioral characteristics of Westerners and Indians are laced with acid wit.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17862490144808929959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
